platform influenced the election.<sup id="cite_ref-npr_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-npr-176">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> He stated that 99% of Facebook\'s content was neither fake news nor a hoax.<sup id="cite_ref-reuters_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters-177">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> Zuckerberg said that Facebook is not a media company.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> Zuckerberg advised users to check the fact-checking website <a href="/wiki/Snopes.com" class="mw-redirect" title="Snopes.com">Snopes.com</a> whenever they encounter fake news on Facebook.<sup id="cite_ref-ohlheiseroutlines_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ohlheiseroutlines-179">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nikitaoutlines_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nikitaoutlines-180">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Top staff members at Facebook did not feel simply blocking ad revenue from fraudulent sites was a strong enough response, and they made an executive decision and created a secret group to deal with the issue themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-hall_174-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hall-174">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-frenkel_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frenkel-175">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> In response to Zuckerberg\'s first statement that fraudulent news did not impact the 2016 election, the secret Facebook group disputed this notion, saying fake news was rampant on their website during the election cycle.<sup id="cite_ref-hall_174-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hall-174">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-frenkel_175-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frenkel-175">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> The secret task force included dozens of Facebook employees.<sup id="cite_ref-hall_174-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hall-174">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-frenkel_175-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frenkel-175">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Response_2">Response</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fake_news_website&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Response">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Facebook faced criticism after its decision to revoke advertising revenues from fraudulent news providers, and not take further action.<sup id="cite_ref-mikeisaac_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mikeisaac-181">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-samuelburke_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-samuelburke-182">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> After negative media coverage including assertions that fraudulent news gave the 2016 U.S. presidential election to Trump, Zuckerberg posted a second time about it on 18 November 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-mikeisaac_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mikeisaac-181">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-samuelburke_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-samuelburke-182">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> The post was a reversal of his earlier comments on the matter where he had discounted the impact of fraudulent news.<sup id="cite_ref-samuelburke_182-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-samuelburke-182">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Zuckerberg said there it was difficult to filter out fraudulent news because he desired open communication.<sup id="cite_ref-mikeisaac_181-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mikeisaac-181">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Measures considered and not implemented by Facebook included adding an ability for users to tag questionable material, automated checking tools, and third-party confirmation.<sup id="cite_ref-mikeisaac_181-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mikeisaac-181">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> The 18 November post did not announce any concrete actions the company would definitively take, or when such measures would be put into usage.<sup id="cite_ref-mikeisaac_181-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mikeisaac-181">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-samuelburke_182-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-samuelburke-182">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/National_Public_Radio" class="mw-redirect" title="National Public Radio">National Public Radio</a> observed the changes being considered by Facebook to identify fraud constituted progress for the company into a new media entity.<sup id="cite_ref-misinformationonfacebook_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-misinformationonfacebook-183">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> On 19 November 2016, BuzzFeed advised Facebook users they could report posts from fraudulent sites.<sup id="cite_ref-howyoucanstop_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-howyoucanstop-184">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Users could choose the report option: "I think it shouldn\'t be on Facebook", followed by: "It\'s a false news story."<sup id="cite_ref-howyoucanstop_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-howyoucanstop-184">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> In November 2016, Facebook began assessing use of warning labels on fake news.<sup id="cite_ref-facebookquietly_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-facebookquietly-185">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> The rollout was at first only available to a few users in a testing phase.<sup id="cite_ref-facebookquietly_185-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-facebookquietly-185">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> A sample warning read: "This website is not a reliable news source. Reason: Classification Pending".<sup id="cite_ref-facebookquietly_185-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-facebookquietly-185">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/TechCrunch" title="TechCrunch">TechCrunch</a></i> analyzed the new feature during the testing phase and surmised it may have a tendency towards <a href="/wiki/False_positive" class="mw-redirect" title="False positive">false positives</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-facebookquietly_185-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-facebookquietly-185">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fake news proliferation on Facebook had a negative financial impact for the company. Brian Wieser of Pivotal Research predicted that revenues could decrease by two percentage points due to the concern over fake news and loss of advertising dollars.<sup id="cite_ref-falsenewsitems_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-falsenewsitems-186">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> Shortly after Mark Zuckerberg\'s second statement on fake news proliferation on his website, Facebook decided to engage in assisting the government of China with a version of its software in the country to allow increased censorship by the government.<sup id="cite_ref-pesek_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pesek-187">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Barron%27s_(newspaper)" title="Barron&#39;s (newspaper)">Barron\'s</a></i> contributor William Pesek was highly critical of this move, writing by porting its fake news conundrum to China, Facebook would become a tool in that <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Communist Party of China">Communist Party</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/General_Secretary_of_the_Communist_Party_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="General Secretary of the Communist Party of China">General Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/Xi_Jinping" title="Xi Jinping">Xi Jinping</a>\'s efforts to increase <a href="/wiki/Censorship_in_China" title="Censorship in China">censorship</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pesek_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pesek-187">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Media scholar Dr. Nolan Higdon argues that relying on tech-companies to solve the issues with false information will exacerbate the problems associated with fake news.<sup id="cite_ref-Higdon_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Higdon-188">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> Higdon contends that tech-companies lack an incentive for solving the problem because they benefit from the proliferation of fake news. Higdon cites tech-companies utilization of data collection as one of the strongest forces empowering fake news producers. Rather than government regulation or industry censorship, Higdon argues for the introduction of critical news literacy education to American education.<sup id="cite_ref-Higdon_188-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Higdon-188">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Partnership_with_debunkers">Partnership with debunkers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fake_news_website&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Partnership with debunkers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Society_of_Professional_Journalists" title="Society of Professional Journalists">Society of Professional Journalists</a> president Lynn Walsh said in November 2016 that they would reach out to Facebook to assist weeding out fake news.<sup id="cite_ref-klasfeld_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-klasfeld-189">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> Walsh said Facebook should evolve and admit it functioned as a media company.<sup id="cite_ref-klasfeld_189-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-klasfeld-189">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> On 17 November 2016, the Poynter International Fact-Checking Network (IFCN)<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup> published an open letter on the <a href="/wiki/Poynter_Institute" title="Poynter Institute">Poynter Institute</a> website to Mark Zuckerberg, imploring him to utilize fact-checkers to identify fraud on Facebook.<sup id="cite_ref-neidigfact_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-neidigfact-193">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-factopenletter_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factopenletter-194">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> Signatories to the 2016 letter to Zuckerberg featured a global representation of fact-checking groups, including: <a href="/wiki/Africa_Check" title="Africa Check">Africa Check</a>, FactCheck.org, PolitiFact.com, and <i>The Washington Post</i> Fact Checker.<sup id="cite_ref-neidigfact_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-neidigfact-193">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-factopenletter_194-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factopenletter-194">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> In his second post on the matter on 18 November 2016, Zuckerberg responded to the fraudulent news problem by suggesting usage of fact-checkers.<sup id="cite_ref-ohlheiseroutlines_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ohlheiseroutlines-179">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nikitaoutlines_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nikitaoutlines-180">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> He specifically identified fact-checking website <a href="/wiki/Snopes.com" class="mw-redirect" title="Snopes.com">Snopes.com</a>, and pointed out that Facebook monitors links to such debunkers in reply comments to determine which original posts were fraudulent.<sup id="cite_ref-ohlheiseroutlines_179-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ohlheiseroutlines-179">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nikitaoutlines_180-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nikitaoutlines-180">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 15 December 2016, Facebook announced more specifics in its efforts to combat fake news and hoaxes on its site.<sup id="cite_ref-facebookdetails_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-facebookdetails-195">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-facebookisturning_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-facebookisturning-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-facebookwillfact_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-facebookwillfact-196">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> The company said it would form a partnership with fact-checking groups that had joined the Poynter International Fact-Checking Network fact-checkers\' code of principles, to help debunk fraud on the site.<sup id="cite_ref-facebookisturning_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-facebookisturning-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-facebookdetails_195-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-facebookdetails-195">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> It was the first instance Facebook had ever given third-party entities highlighted featuring in its <a href="/wiki/News_Feed" title="News Feed">News Feed</a>, a significant motivator of web traffic online.<sup id="cite_ref-facebookisturning_32-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-facebookisturning-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The fact-checking organizations partnered with Facebook in order to confirm whether or not links posted from one individual to another on the site were factual or fraudulent.<sup id="cite_ref-facebookisturning_32-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-facebookisturning-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Facebook did not finance the fact-checkers, and acknowledged they could see increased traffic to their sites from the partnership.<sup id="cite_ref-facebookisturning_32-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-facebookisturning-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fact-checking organizations that joined Facebook\'s initiative included: <a href="/wiki/ABC_News" title="ABC News">ABC News</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Snopes.com" class="mw-redirect" title="Snopes.com">Snopes.com</a>, <a href="/wiki/FactCheck.org" title="FactCheck.org">FactCheck.org</a>, <a href="/wiki/PolitiFact" title="PolitiFact">PolitiFact</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-facebookisturning_32-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-facebookisturning-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Fraudulent articles will receive a warning tag: "disputed by 3rd party fact-checkers".<sup id="cite_ref-facebookdetails_195-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-facebookdetails-195">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> The company planned to start with obvious cases of hoaxes shared specifically for fraudulent purposes to gain money for the purveyor of fake news.<sup id="cite_ref-facebookisturning_32-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-facebookisturning-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Users may still share such tagged articles, and they will show up farther down in the news feed with an accompanying warning.<sup id="cite_ref-facebookdetails_195-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-facebookdetails-195">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> Facebook will employ staff researchers to determine whether <a href="/wiki/Website_spoofing" title="Website spoofing">website spoofing</a> has occurred, for example "washingtonpost.co" instead of the real washingtonpost.com.<sup id="cite_ref-facebookwillfact_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-facebookwillfact-196">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> In a post on 15 December, Mark Zuckerberg acknowledged the changing nature of Facebook: "I think of Facebook as a technology company, but I recognize we have a greater responsibility than just building technology that information flows through. While we don\'t write the news stories you read and share, we also recognize we\'re more than just a distributor of news. We\'re a new kind of platform for public discourse -- and that means we have a new kind of responsibility to enable people to have the most meaningful conversations, and to build a space where people can be informed."<sup id="cite_ref-facebookwillfact_196-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-facebookwillfact-196">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_technology_tools">Proposed technology tools</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fake_news_website&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Proposed technology tools">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><i><a href="/wiki/New_York_(magazine)" title="New York (magazine)">New York</a></i> magazine contributor Brian Feldman created a <a href="/wiki/Google_Chrome_extension" class="mw-redirect" title="Google Chrome extension">Google Chrome extension</a> that would warn users about fraudulent news sites.<sup id="cite_ref-brianfeldman_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brianfeldman-197">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> He invited others to use his code and improve upon it.<sup id="cite_ref-brianfeldman_197-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brianfeldman-197">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Upworthy" title="Upworthy">Upworthy</a> co-founder and <i>The Filter Bubble</i> author <a href="/wiki/Eli_Pariser" title="Eli Pariser">Eli Pariser</a> launched an <a href="/wiki/Open-source_model" title="Open-source model">open-source model</a> initiative on 17 November 2016 to address false news.<sup id="cite_ref-parisercrowdsourced_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parisercrowdsourced-198">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hivemindassemble_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hivemindassemble-199">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> Pariser began a <a href="/wiki/Google_Docs,_Sheets_and_Slides" class="mw-redirect" title="Google Docs, Sheets and Slides">Google Document</a> to collaborate with others online on how to lessen the phenomenon of fraudulent news.<sup id="cite_ref-parisercrowdsourced_198-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parisercrowdsourced-198">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hivemindassemble_199-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hivemindassemble-199">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> Pariser called his initiative: "Design Solutions for Fake News".<sup id="cite_ref-parisercrowdsourced_198-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parisercrowdsourced-198">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> Pariser\'s document included recommendations for a ratings organization analogous to the <a href="/wiki/Better_Business_Bureau" title="Better Business Bureau">Better Business Bureau</a>, and a database on media producers in a format like <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia" title="Wikipedia">Wikipedia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-parisercrowdsourced_198-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parisercrowdsourced-198">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hivemindassemble_199-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hivemindassemble-199">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> Writing for <i>Fortune</i>, Matthew Ingram agreed with the idea that Wikipedia could serve as a helpful model to improve Facebook\'s analysis of potentially fake news.<sup id="cite_ref-facebookdoesntneed_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-facebookdoesntneed-200">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> Ingram concluded Facebook could benefit from a social network form of fact-checking similar to Wikipedia\'s methods while incorporating debunking websites such as <a href="/wiki/PolitiFact.com" class="mw-redirect" title="PolitiFact.com">PolitiFact.com</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-facebookdoesntneed_200-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-facebookdoesntneed-200">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Others">Others</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fake_news_website&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Others">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Pope_Francis" title="Pope Francis">Pope Francis</a>, the leader of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a>, spoke out against fake news in an interview with the Belgian Catholic weekly <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tertio_(magazine)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Tertio (magazine) (page does not exist)">Tertio (magazine)</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://nl.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tertio" class="extiw" title="nl:Tertio">nl</a>&#93;</span></i> on 7 December 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-popewarnsnyt_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-popewarnsnyt-201">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> The Pope had prior experience being the subject of a fake news website fiction\xe2\x80\x94during the 2016 U.S. election cycle, he was falsely said to support Donald Trump for president.<sup id="cite_ref-popewarnsnyt_201-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-popewarnsnyt-201">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-alyssanewcomb_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alyssanewcomb-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-didthepope_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-didthepope-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Pope Francis said the singular worst thing the news media could do was spreading <a href="/wiki/Disinformation" title="Disinformation">disinformation</a> and that amplifying fake news instead of educating society was a <a href="/wiki/Sin" title="Sin">sin</a>. He compared salacious reporting of scandals, whether true or not, to <a href="/wiki/Coprophilia" title="Coprophilia">coprophilia</a> and the consumption of it to <a href="/wiki/Coprophagy" class="mw-redirect" title="Coprophagy">coprophagy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-popewarnsreuters_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-popewarnsreuters-202">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-popefranciscompares_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-popefranciscompares-203">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-harrietsherwood_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harrietsherwood-204">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-andrewgriffin_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-andrewgriffin-205">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> The Pope said that he did not intend to offend with his strong words, but emphasized that "a lot of damage can be done" when the truth is disregarded and slander is spread.<sup id="cite_ref-popefranciscompares_203-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-popefranciscompares-203">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-andrewgriffin_205-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-andrewgriffin-205">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Academic_analysis">Academic analysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fake_news_website&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Academic analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Jamie Condliffe wrote that banning ad revenue from fraudulent sites was not aggressive enough action by Facebook to deal with the problem, and did not prevent fake news from appearing in Facebook news feeds.<sup id="cite_ref-condliffe_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-condliffe-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> University of Michigan political scientist <a href="/wiki/Brendan_Nyhan" title="Brendan Nyhan">Brendan Nyhan</a> criticized Facebook for not doing more to combat fake news amplification.<sup id="cite_ref-viralfakeelection_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-viralfakeelection-206">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Indiana_University_Bloomington" title="Indiana University Bloomington">Indiana University</a> <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a> professor <a href="/wiki/Filippo_Menczer" title="Filippo Menczer">Filippo Menczer</a> commented on measures by Google and Facebook to deny fraudulent sites revenue, saying it was a good step to reduce motivation for fraudsters.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> Menczer\'s research team engaged in developing an online tool titled: Hoaxy \xe2\x80\x94 to see the pervasiveness of unconfirmed assertions as well as related debunking on the Internet.<sup id="cite_ref-menczer_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-menczer-208">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zeynep_Tufekci_2_(5805394290).jpg" class="image"><img alt="Zeynep Tufekci, a writer and academic" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Zeynep_Tufekci_2_%285805394290%29.jpg/220px-Zeynep_Tufekci_2_%285805394290%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Zeynep_Tufekci_2_%285805394290%29.jpg/330px-Zeynep_Tufekci_2_%285805394290%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Zeynep_Tufekci_2_%285805394290%29.jpg/440px-Zeynep_Tufekci_2_%285805394290%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3861" data-file-height="2574" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zeynep_Tufekci_2_(5805394290).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Zeynep_Tufekci" title="Zeynep Tufekci">Zeynep Tufekci</a> has written that Facebook amplified fake news and <a href="/wiki/Echo_chamber_(media)" title="Echo chamber (media)">echo chambers</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Zeynep_Tufekci" title="Zeynep Tufekci">Zeynep Tufekci</a> wrote critically about Facebook\'s stance on fraudulent news sites, stating that fraudulent websites in Macedonia profited handsomely off false stories about the 2016 U.S. election.<sup id="cite_ref-trytodrain_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trytodrain-209">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> Tufecki wrote that Facebook\'s algorithms, and structure exacerbated the impact of <a href="/wiki/Echo_chamber_(media)" title="Echo chamber (media)">echo chambers</a> and increased fake news blight.<sup id="cite_ref-trytodrain_209-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trytodrain-209">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2016 Melissa Zimdars, associate professor of communications at Merrimack College,<sup id="cite_ref-merrimack01_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merrimack01-210">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> created a handout for her Introduction to Mass Communication students titled "False, Misleading, Clickbait-y, and/or Satirical \'News\' Sources" and posted it on Google docs.<sup id="cite_ref-ZimdarsNov2016_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ZimdarsNov2016-211">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> It was circulated on social media, and on 15 November 2016 <i>The Los Angeles Times</i> published the class handout under the title "Want to keep fake news out of your newsfeed? College professor creates list of sites to avoid".<sup id="cite_ref-LATimes2016_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LATimes2016-212">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> Zimdars said that the list "wasn\'t intended to be widely distributed", and expressed concern that "people are taking it as this list of \'fake\' sites, which is not its purpose". On 17 November 2016 Zimdars deleted the list.<sup id="cite_ref-USAToday2016_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USAToday2016-213">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> On 3 January 2017, Zimdars replaced the original handout with a new list at the same URL.<sup id="cite_ref-ZimdarsNov2017_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ZimdarsNov2017-214">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> The new list has removed most of the sites from the original handout, added many new sites, and greatly expanded the categories. \n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a> professors Sam Wineburg and Sarah McGrew authored a 2016 study analyzing students\' ability to discern fraudulent news from factual.<sup id="cite_ref-canyoutell_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-canyoutell-215">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-stanfordstudy_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stanfordstudy-216">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> The study took place over a year-long period of time, and involved a sample size of over 7,800 responses from university, secondary and middle school students in 12 states within the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-canyoutell_215-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-canyoutell-215">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-stanfordstudy_216-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stanfordstudy-216">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> They were surprised at the consistency with which students thought fraudulent news reports were factual.<sup id="cite_ref-canyoutell_215-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-canyoutell-215">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-stanfordstudy_216-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stanfordstudy-216">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> The study found 82% of students in <a href="/wiki/Middle_school" title="Middle school">middle school</a> were unable to differentiate between an advertisement denoted as <a href="/wiki/Sponsored_content" class="mw-redirect" title="Sponsored content">sponsored content</a> from an actual news article.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> The authors concluded the solution was to educate online media consumers to themselves behave like fact-checkers \xe2\x80\x94 and actively question the veracity of all sources.<sup id="cite_ref-canyoutell_215-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-canyoutell-215">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-stanfordstudy_216-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stanfordstudy-216">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> A 2019 study in the journal <i>Science</i>, which examined dissemination of fake news articles on Facebook in the 2016 election, found that sharing of fake news articles on Facebook was "relatively rare", conservatives were more likely than liberals or moderates to share fake news, and there is a "strong age effect", whereby individuals over 65 are vastly more likely to share fake news than younger cohorts.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> Another 2019 study in <i>Science</i> found, "fake news accounted for nearly 6% of all news consumption [on Twitter], but it was heavily concentrated\xe2\x80\x94only 1% of users were exposed to 80% of fake news, and 0.1% of users were responsible for sharing 80% of fake news. Interestingly, fake news was most concentrated among conservative voters."<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Scientist <a href="/wiki/Emily_Willingham" title="Emily Willingham">Emily Willingham</a> has proposed applying the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a> to fake news analysis.<sup id="cite_ref-willingham_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-willingham-220">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> She had previously written on the topic of differentiating science from <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudoscience</a>, and proposed applying that logic to fake news.<sup id="cite_ref-willingham_220-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-willingham-220">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> She calls the recommended steps Observe, Question, Hypothesize, Analyze data, Draw conclusion, and Act on results.<sup id="cite_ref-willingham_220-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-willingham-220">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> Willingham suggested a <a href="/wiki/Hypothesis" title="Hypothesis">hypothesis</a> of "This is real news", and then forming a strong set of questions to attempt to disprove the hypothesis.<sup id="cite_ref-willingham_220-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-willingham-220">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> These tests included: check the <a href="/wiki/URL" title="URL">URL</a>, date of the article, evaluate reader bias and writer bias, double-check the evidence, and verify the sources cited.<sup id="cite_ref-willingham_220-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-willingham-220">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/University_of_Connecticut" title="University of Connecticut">University of Connecticut</a> philosophy professor <a href="/wiki/Michael_P._Lynch" title="Michael P. Lynch">Michael P. Lynch</a> said that a troubling number of individuals make determinations relying upon the most recent piece of information they\'ve consumed.<sup id="cite_ref-tavernise_35-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tavernise-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> He said the greater issue however was that fake news could make people less likely to believe news that really is true.<sup id="cite_ref-tavernise_35-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tavernise-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Lynch summed up the thought process of such individuals, as "...ignore the facts because nobody knows what\xe2\x80\x99s really true anyway."<sup id="cite_ref-tavernise_35-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tavernise-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Journalistic_objectivity" title="Journalistic objectivity">Journalistic objectivity</a></div>\n<p>In 2019, <a href="/wiki/David_Lazer" title="David Lazer">David Lazer</a> and other researchers, from <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_University" title="Northeastern University">Northeastern University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/University_at_Buffalo" title="University at Buffalo">University at Buffalo</a>, analyzed engagement with a previously defined set of fake news sources on Twitter. They found that such engagement was highly concentrated both among a small number of websites and a small number of Twitter users. Five percent of the sources accounted for over fifty percent of exposures. Among users, 0.1 percent consumed eighty percent of the volume from fake news sources.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fake_news_website&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output title="Yellow journalism">Yellow journalism</a></li></ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Footnotes">Footnotes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fake_news_website&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Footnotes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-67"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-67">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i><a href="/wiki/Fortune_(magazine)" title="Fortune (magazine)">Fortune magazine</a></i> described the <a href="/wiki/Foreign_Policy_Research_Institute" title="Foreign Policy Research Institute">Foreign Policy Research Institute</a> as: "a conservative think tank known for its generally hawkish stance on relations between the U.S. and Russia"<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-159"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-159">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The Fact Checker" is a project by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i> to analyze political claims.<sup id="cite_ref-stelterfact_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stelterfact-158">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Their colleagues and competitors at <a href="/wiki/FactCheck.org" title="FactCheck.org">FactCheck.org</a> recommended The Fact Checker as a resource to use before assuming a story is factual.<sup id="cite_ref-factcheckhowtospot_156-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factcheckhowtospot-156">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-192"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-192">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Created in September 2015, the Poynter International Fact-Checking Network (IFCN) is housed within the <a href="/wiki/St._Petersburg,_Florida" title="St. Petersburg, Florida">St. Petersburg, Florida</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Poynter_Institute_for_Media_Studies" class="mw-redirect" title="Poynter Institute for Media Studies">Poynter Institute for Media Studies</a> and aims to support the work of 64 member fact-checking organizations around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-kristenhare_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kristenhare-190">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-aboutifcn_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aboutifcn-191">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> Alexios Mantzarlis, co-founder of FactCheckEU.org and former managing editor of Italian fact-checking site Pagella Politica, was named director and editor of IFCN in September 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-kristenhare_190-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kristenhare-190">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-aboutifcn_191-1" class="reference"><a
